<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Deepwater Horizon</i> oil spill</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>

<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em"><caption>External video</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://video.pbs.org/video/1625293496/">Frontline: The Spill</a> (54:25), <a href="/wiki/Frontline_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Frontline (U.S. TV series)">Frontline</a> on <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a><sup id="cite_ref-PBS_A_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS_A-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b><i>Deepwater Horizon</i> oil spill</b> (also referred to as the <b>BP oil spill</b>/<b>leak</b>, the <b>BP oil disaster</b>, the <b>Gulf of Mexico oil spill</b>, and the <b>Macondo blowout)</b> is an industrial disaster that began on 20 April 2010, in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> on the <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a>-operated <a href="/wiki/Macondo_Prospect" title="Macondo Prospect">Macondo Prospect</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-nyt020810_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt020810-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-4_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-4-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-largest_in_US_hist_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-largest_in_US_hist-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-5_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-5-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> considered to be the largest marine <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spill</a> in the history of the petroleum industry and estimated to be 8% to 31% larger in volume than the previous largest, the <a href="/wiki/Ixtoc_I_oil_spill" title="Ixtoc I oil spill">Ixtoc I oil spill</a>. The U.S. government estimated the total discharge at 4.9 million barrels (210&#160;million US&#160;gal; 780,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-report2011_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-report2011Live_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011Live-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>After several failed efforts to contain the flow, the well was declared sealed on 19 September 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Aspress_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aspress-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Reports in early 2012 indicated that the well site was still leaking.<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-3_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-3-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A massive response ensued to protect beaches, <a href="/wiki/Salt_marsh" title="Salt marsh">wetlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a> from the spreading oil utilizing skimmer ships, floating <a href="/wiki/Boom_(containment)" title="Boom (containment)">booms</a>, controlled burns and 1.84&#160;million US gallons (7,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of <a href="/wiki/Oil_dispersant" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil dispersant">oil dispersant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-staff4_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-staff4-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Due to the months-long spill, along with adverse effects from the response and cleanup activities, extensive damage to marine and wildlife habitats and fishing and tourism industries was reported.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-7_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-7-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nation180412_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nation180412-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>   In Louisiana, 4,900,000 pounds (2,200&#160;t) of oily material was removed from the beaches in 2013, over double the amount collected in 2012. Oil cleanup crews worked four days a week on 55 miles (89&#160;km) of Louisiana shoreline throughout 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated7_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated7-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Oil continued to be found as far from the Macondo site as the waters off the Florida Panhandle and Tampa Bay, where scientists said the oil and dispersant mixture is embedded in the sand.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  In April 2013, it was reported that <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> and other marine life continued to die in record numbers with infant dolphins dying at six times the normal rate.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  One study released in 2014 reported that tuna and <a href="/wiki/Amberjack" title="Amberjack">amberjack</a> that were exposed to oil from the spill developed deformities of the heart and other organs that would be expected to be fatal or at least life-shortening and another study found that <a href="/wiki/Cardiotoxicity" title="Cardiotoxicity">cardiotoxicity</a> might have been widespread in animal life exposed to the spill.<sup id="cite_ref-LATimes-fish-toxins_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-fish-toxins-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wines-24-March-14_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wines-24-March-14-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous investigations explored the causes of the explosion and record-setting spill.  The U.S. government September 2011 report pointed to defective cement on the well, faulting mostly BP, but also rig operator <a href="/wiki/Transocean" title="Transocean">Transocean</a> and contractor <a href="/wiki/Halliburton" title="Halliburton">Halliburton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BOERMEPR_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOERMEPR-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-17_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-17-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Earlier in 2011, a White House commission likewise blamed BP and its partners for a series of cost-cutting decisions and an inadequate safety system, but also concluded that the spill resulted from "systemic" root causes and "absent significant reform in both industry practices and government policies, might well recur".<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-16_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-16-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2012, BP and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> settled federal criminal charges with BP pleading guilty to 11 counts of <a href="/wiki/Manslaughter" title="Manslaughter">manslaughter</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Misdemeanors" class="mw-redirect" title="Misdemeanors">misdemeanors</a>, and a felony count of lying to Congress.  BP also agreed to four years of government monitoring of its safety practices and ethics, and the Environmental Protection Agency announced that BP would be temporarily banned from new contracts with the US government.  BP and the Department of Justice agreed to a record-setting $4.525&#160;billion in fines and other payments.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt151112_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt151112-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-latimes290113_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes290113-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-21_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-21-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>  As of February 2013, criminal and civil settlements and payments to a trust fund had cost the company $42.2 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2014, a U.S. District Court judge ruled that BP was primarily responsible for the oil spill because of its gross negligence and reckless conduct.<sup id="cite_ref-Times_-_barbier_-_18_billion_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times_-_barbier_-_18_billion-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In July 2015, BP agreed to pay $18.7 billion in fines, the largest corporate settlement in U.S. history.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>How the misuse of <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Policy_Act#Preparation_of_a_Categorical_Exclusion" title="National Environmental Policy Act">categorical exclusion</a> for <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Policy_Act" title="National Environmental Policy Act">National Environmental Policy Act</a> (NEPA) compliance relates to this spill is described in <a href="/wiki/Regulatory_capture#Bureau_of_Ocean_Energy_Management,_Regulation_and_Enforcement" title="Regulatory capture">Regulatory Capture</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deepwater_Horizon_drilling_rig"><i>Deepwater Horizon</i> drilling rig</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Deepwater Horizon drilling rig">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon" title="Deepwater Horizon">Deepwater Horizon</a></div>
<div class="noviewer thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px"><div style="position:relative;width:250px;border:1px solid lightgray"><a href="/wiki/File:Usa_edcp_location_map.svg" class="image" title="Origin of oil spill"><img alt="Origin of oil spill" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Usa_edcp_location_map.svg/250px-Usa_edcp_location_map.svg.png" width="250" height="155" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Usa_edcp_location_map.svg/375px-Usa_edcp_location_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Usa_edcp_location_map.svg/500px-Usa_edcp_location_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1181" data-file-height="731" /></a><div style="position:absolute;top:83.199%;left:64.525%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><img alt="Origin of oil spill" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/8px-Red_pog.svg.png" title="Deepwater Horizon oil spill" width="8" height="8" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/12px-Red_pog.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/16px-Red_pog.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Usa_edcp_location_map.svg" title="File:Usa edcp location map.svg"> </a></div>Location of the <i>Deepwater Horizon</i> on 20 April 2010</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Deepwater_Horizon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e0/Deepwater_Horizon.jpg/220px-Deepwater_Horizon.jpg" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e0/Deepwater_Horizon.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="310" data-file-height="261" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deepwater_Horizon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Deepwater Horizon</i> prior to explosion.</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon" title="Deepwater Horizon">Deepwater Horizon</a></i> was a 10-year-old<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Semi-submersible#Mobile_offshore_drilling_units_(MODU)" title="Semi-submersible">semi-submersible, mobile, floating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_positioning" title="Dynamic positioning">dynamically positioned</a> <a href="/wiki/Drilling_rig" title="Drilling rig">drilling rig</a> that could operate in waters up to 10,000 feet (3,000&#160;m) deep.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-22_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-22-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Built by South Korean company <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_Heavy_Industries" title="Hyundai Heavy Industries">Hyundai Heavy Industries</a><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-23_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-23-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and owned by <a href="/wiki/Transocean" title="Transocean">Transocean</a>, the rig operated under the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Islands" title="Marshall Islands">Marshallese</a> <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_convenience" title="Flag of convenience">flag of convenience</a>, and was chartered to BP from March 2008 to September 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-MHL_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MHL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It was drilling a deep exploratory well, 18,360 feet (5,600&#160;m) below sea level, in approximately 5,100 feet (1,600&#160;m) of water. The well is situated in the <a href="/wiki/Macondo_Prospect" title="Macondo Prospect">Macondo Prospect</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Canyon" title="Mississippi Canyon">Mississippi Canyon</a> Block 252 (MC252) of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, in the United States' <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zone</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Macondo_well" class="mw-redirect" title="Macondo well">Macondo well</a> is found roughly 41 miles (66&#160;km) off the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> coast.<sup id="cite_ref-USA_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USA-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BP_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> BP was the operator and principal developer of the Macondo Prospect with a 65% share, while 25% was owned by <a href="/wiki/Anadarko_Petroleum_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Anadarko Petroleum Corporation">Anadarko Petroleum Corporation</a>, and 10% by <a href="/wiki/Mitsui_Oil_Exploration_Co.#United_States_operations" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitsui Oil Exploration Co.">MOEX Offshore 2007</a>, a unit of <a href="/wiki/Mitsui" title="Mitsui">Mitsui</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-subsea_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-subsea-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Explosion">Explosion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Explosion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;523864&quot; data-framerate=&quot;29.97002997003&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Deepwater Horizon fire seen by US Coast Guard helicopter.ogv" style="width:220px;height:165px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Deepwater_Horizon_fire_seen_by_US_Coast_Guard_helicopter.ogv/220px--Deepwater_Horizon_fire_seen_by_US_Coast_Guard_helicopter.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/Deepwater_Horizon_fire_seen_by_US_Coast_Guard_helicopter.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Deepwater_Horizon_fire_seen_by_US_Coast_Guard_helicopter.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Supply boats continued to battle the fire, viewed from a Coast Guard helicopter</div></div></div>
<p>At approximately <span class="nowrap">9:45&#160;pm <a href="/wiki/Central_Time_Zone_(Americas)" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Time Zone (Americas)">CDT</a></span>, on 20 April 2010, high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> gas from the well expanded into the <a href="/wiki/Marine_riser" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine riser">drilling riser</a> and rose into the drilling rig, where it ignited and exploded, engulfing the platform.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream220410_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream220410-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yahoo1_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yahoo1-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> At the time, 126 crew members were on board: seven BP employees, 79 of Transocean, and employees of various other companies.<sup id="cite_ref-mail180610_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mail180610-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>  Eleven missing workers were never found despite a three-day <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">U.S. Coast Guard</a> (USCG) search operation and are believed to have died in the explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-Times_search_end_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times_search_end-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-huffington151112a_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffington151112a-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Ninety-four crew members were rescued by <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(shipboard)" title="Lifeboat (shipboard)">lifeboat</a> or helicopter, 17 of whom were treated for injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream220410_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream220410-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nola_0422_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola_0422-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The Deepwater Horizon sank on the morning of 22 April 2010.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Volume_and_extent_of_oil_spill">Volume and extent of oil spill</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Volume and extent of oil spill">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Volume_and_extent_of_the_Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Volume and extent of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Volume and extent of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Timeline of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Timeline of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg/220px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg/330px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg/440px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6436W-023.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil from the Deepwater Horizon oil spill approaches the coast of Mobile, Alabama, 6 May 2010</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg/220px-Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg/330px-Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg/440px-Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4204" data-file-height="2765" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Stained_Beaches.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil stained beaches in Pensacola, Florida; 1 July 2010</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg/220px-Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg/330px-Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg/440px-Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Warzone_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burning and skimming operations in the Gulf of Mexico; 10 June 2010</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg/220px-Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg/330px-Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg/440px-Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thick_Oil_Washes_Ashore.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thick oil washes ashore in Louisiana; 10 June 2010</div></div></div>
<p>The oil leak was discovered on the afternoon of 22 April 2010 when a large oil slick began to spread at the former rig site.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-24_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-24-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The oil flowed for 87 days. BP originally estimated a flow rate of 1,000 to 5,000 barrels per day (160 to 790&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d). The <a href="/wiki/Flow_Rate_Technical_Group" title="Flow Rate Technical Group">Flow Rate Technical Group</a> (FRTG) estimated the initial flow rate was 62,000 barrels per day (9,900&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d).<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_29/4_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_29/4-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-28_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-28-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AP_60k_barrels_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP_60k_barrels-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The total estimated volume of leaked oil approximated <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(volume)#Terrestrial" title="Orders of magnitude (volume)">4.9 million barrels (210&#160;million US&#160;gal; 780,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>)</a> with plus or minus 10% uncertainty,<sup id="cite_ref-report2011_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-report2011Live_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011Live-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>including oil that was collected,<sup id="cite_ref-ap110113_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap110113-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> making it the world’s largest accidental spill.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt020810_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt020810-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hoch_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoch-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> BP challenged the higher figure, saying that the government overestimated the volume. Internal emails released in 2013 showed that one BP employee had estimates that matched those of the FRTG, and shared the data with supervisors, but BP continued with their lower number.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oilprice_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oilprice-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The company argued that government figures do not reflect over 810,000 barrels (34&#160;million US&#160;gal; 129,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil that was collected or burned before it could enter the Gulf waters.<sup id="cite_ref-ap110113_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap110113-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the satellite images, the spill directly affected 68,000 square miles (180,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of ocean, which is comparable to the size of <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-area_of_spill_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-area_of_spill-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-norse_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norse-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> By early June 2010, oil had washed up on 125 miles (201&#160;km) of Louisiana's coast and along the Mississippi, Florida, and Alabama coastlines.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-49_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-49-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-50_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-50-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Oil sludge appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Intracoastal_Waterway" title="Intracoastal Waterway">Intracoastal Waterway</a> and on <a href="/wiki/Pensacola_Beach" class="mw-redirect" title="Pensacola Beach">Pensacola Beach</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Islands_National_Seashore" title="Gulf Islands National Seashore">Gulf Islands National Seashore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kunzelman_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kunzelman-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In late June, oil reached <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Park_Estates,_Mississippi" title="Gulf Park Estates, Mississippi">Gulf Park Estates</a>, its first appearance in Mississippi.<sup id="cite_ref-McConnaughey_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McConnaughey-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In July, <a href="/wiki/Tarball_(oil)" title="Tarball (oil)">tar balls</a> reached <a href="/wiki/Grand_Isle,_Louisiana" title="Grand Isle, Louisiana">Grand Isle</a> and the shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lozano_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lozano-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-51_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-51-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In September a new wave of oil suddenly coated 16 miles (26&#160;km) of Louisiana coastline and marshes west of the Mississippi River in <a href="/wiki/Plaquemines_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Plaquemines Parish, Louisiana">Plaquemines Parish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-52_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-52-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In October, weathered oil reached Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-53_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-53-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> As of July 2011, about 491 miles (790&#160;km) of coastline in Louisiana, Mississippi, Alabama and Florida were contaminated by oil and a total of 1,074 miles (1,728&#160;km) had been oiled since the spill began.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-12_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-12-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> As of December 2012, 339 miles (546&#160;km) of coastline remain subject to evaluation and/or cleanup operations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramseur_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramseur-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerns were raised about the appearance of underwater, horizontally extended plumes of dissolved oil. Researchers concluded that deep plumes of dissolved oil and gas would likely remain confined to the northern Gulf of Mexico and that the peak impact on dissolved oxygen would be delayed and long lasting.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-62_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-62-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
Two weeks after the wellhead was capped on 15 July 2010, the surface oil appeared to have dissipated, while an unknown amount of subsurface oil remained.<sup id="cite_ref-GillisRobertson_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GillisRobertson-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Estimates of the residual ranged from a 2010 NOAA report that claimed about half of the oil remained below the surface to independent estimates of up to 75%.<sup id="cite_ref-Talev_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Talev-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-43_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-43-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-44_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-44-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>That means that over 100&#160;million US gallons (380&#160;Ml) (2.4 million barrels) remained in the Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramseur_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramseur-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> As of January 2011, tar balls, oil sheen trails, fouled wetlands marsh grass and coastal sands were still evident. Subsurface oil remained offshore and in fine silts.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-10_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-10-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In April 2012, oil was still found along as much as 200 miles (320&#160;km) of Louisiana coastline and tar balls continued to wash up on the barrier islands.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-15_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-15-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, some scientists at the Gulf of Mexico Oil Spill and Ecosystem Science Conference said that as much as one-third of the oil may have mixed with deep ocean sediments, where it risks damage to ecosystems and commercial fisheries.<sup id="cite_ref-nature260113_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature260113-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, more than 4,600,000 pounds (2,100&#160;t) of "oiled material" was removed from the Louisiana coast.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated7_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated7-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Although only "minute" quantities of oil continued to wash up in 2013, patches of tar balls were still being reported almost every day from Alabama and Florida Panhandle beaches. Regular cleanup patrols were no longer considered justified but cleanup was being conducted on an as-needed basis, in response to public reports.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was first thought that oil had not reached as far as <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay,_Florida" class="mw-redirect" title="Tampa Bay, Florida">Tampa Bay, Florida</a>; however, a study done in 2013 found that one of the plumes of dispersant-treated oil had reached a shelf 80 miles (130&#160;km) off the Tampa Bay region.  According to researchers, there is "some evidence it may have caused lesions in fish caught in that area".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efforts_to_stem_the_flow_of_oil">Efforts to stem the flow of oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Efforts to stem the flow of oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Efforts_to_stem_the_Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Efforts to stem the Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Efforts to stem the Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Offshore_oil_spill_prevention_and_response" title="Offshore oil spill prevention and response">Offshore oil spill prevention and response</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Short-term_efforts">Short-term efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Short-term efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg/220px-BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg/330px-BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg/440px-BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="490" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BP_oil_containment_domes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Concept diagram of underwater oil containment domes originally planned for the Deepwater Horizon oil spill. At this stage, there were 2 remaining oil leaks from the fallen pipeline.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg/170px-Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg/255px-Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg/340px-Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2790" data-file-height="4201" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_containment_chamber_Port_Fourchon_LA_100426-G-8744K-021.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil <a href="/wiki/Containment_dome" title="Containment dome">containment dome</a> under construction in Port Fourchon, Louisiana, at <a href="/wiki/Wild_Well_Control" title="Wild Well Control">Wild Well Control</a> on 26 April</div></div></div>
<p>First BP unsuccessfully attempted to close the <a href="/wiki/Blowout_preventer" title="Blowout preventer">blowout preventer</a> valves on the wellhead with <a href="/wiki/Remotely_operated_vehicle" title="Remotely operated vehicle">remotely operated underwater vehicles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-leakfix_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leakfix-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_02/5_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_02/5-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Next it placed a 125-tonne (280,000&#160;lb) containment dome over the largest leak and piped the oil to a storage vessel. While this technique had worked in shallower water, it failed here when gas combined with cold water to form <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane hydrate</a> crystals that blocked the opening at the top of the dome.<sup id="cite_ref-thestar1405_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thestar1405-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Pumping heavy <a href="/wiki/Drilling_fluid" title="Drilling fluid">drilling fluids</a> into the blowout preventer to restrict the flow of oil before sealing it permanently with cement ("<a href="/wiki/Top_kill" title="Top kill">top kill</a>") also failed.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-80_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-80-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-81_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-81-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>BP then inserted a riser insertion tube into the pipe and a stopper-like washer around the tube plugged at the end of the riser and diverted the flow into the insertion tube.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream150510_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream150510-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The collected gas was flared and oil stored on board the <a href="/wiki/Drillship" title="Drillship">drillship</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Discoverer_Enterprise" title="Discoverer Enterprise">Discoverer Enterprise</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-bp240510_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bp240510-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Before the tube was removed, it collected 924,000 US gallons (22,000&#160;bbl; 3,500&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-82_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-82-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> On 3 June 2010, BP removed the damaged <a href="/wiki/Drilling_riser" title="Drilling riser">drilling riser</a> from the top of the blowout preventer and covered the pipe by the cap which connected it to another riser.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-83_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-83-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> On 16 June a second containment system connected directly to the blowout preventer began carrying oil and gas to service vessels, where it was consumed in a clean-burning system.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream170610_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream170610-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The United States government's estimates suggested the cap and other equipment were capturing less than half of the leaking oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Kunzelman_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kunzelman-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> On 10 July the containment cap was removed to replace it with a better-fitting cap ("Top Hat Number 10").<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-84_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-84-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-85_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-85-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Mud and cement were later pumped in through the top of the well to reduce the pressure inside it which didn't work either. A final device was created to attach a chamber of larger diameter than the flowing pipe with a flange that bolted to the top of the blowout preventer and a manual valve set to close off the flow once attached. On 15 July the device was secured and time was taken closing the valves to ensure the attachment under increasing pressure until the valves were closed completing the temporary measures.<sup id="cite_ref-Yahoo7-20100715_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yahoo7-20100715-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Explosives">Explosives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Explosives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In mid-May, United States Secretary of Energy <a href="/wiki/Steven_Chu" title="Steven Chu">Steven Chu</a> assembled a team of nuclear physicists, including <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a> designer <a href="/wiki/Richard_Garwin" title="Richard Garwin">Richard Garwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> director Tom Hunter.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-86_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-86-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Oil expert <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Simmons" title="Matthew Simmons">Matthew Simmons</a> maintained that a nuclear explosion was the only way BP could permanently seal the well and cited successful Soviet attempts to seal off runaway gas wells with nuclear blasts. A spokesperson for the U.S. Energy Department said that "neither Energy Secretary Steven Chu nor anyone else" ever considered this option.<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc020610_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc020610-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-raw290510_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raw290510-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> On 24 May BP ruled out conventional explosives, claiming that if blasts failed to clog the well, "we would have denied ourselves all other options."<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-87_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-87-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Well_declared_.22effectively_dead.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Well_declared_&quot;effectively_dead&quot;">Well declared "effectively dead"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Well declared &quot;effectively dead&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Transocean's <i><a href="/wiki/Development_Driller_III" title="Development Driller III">Development Driller III</a></i> started drilling a first <a href="/wiki/Relief_well" title="Relief well">relief well</a> on 2 May 2010. <i><a href="/wiki/GSF_Development_Driller_II" title="GSF Development Driller II">GSF Development Driller II</a></i> started drilling a second relief on 16 May 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-relief_map_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-relief_map-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-upstream170510_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream170510-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-90_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-90-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> On 3 August 2010, first test oil and then drilling mud was pumped at a slow rate of approximately 2 barrels (320&#160;L) per minute into the well-head. Pumping continued for eight hours, at the end of which time the well was declared to be "in a static condition."<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-91_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-91-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> On 4 August 2010, BP began pumping cement from the top, sealing that part of the flow channel permanently.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-92_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-92-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 3 September 2010, the 300-<a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">ton</a> failed blowout preventer was removed from the well and a replacement blowout preventer was installed.<sup id="cite_ref-20100903BlowoutPreventerRemoved_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20100903BlowoutPreventerRemoved-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20100904BlowoutPreventerOnBoat_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20100904BlowoutPreventerOnBoat-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> On 16 September 2010, the relief well reached its destination and pumping of cement to seal the well began.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-93_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-93-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> On 19 September 2010, <a href="/wiki/National_Incident_Commander" class="mw-redirect" title="National Incident Commander">National Incident Commander</a> <a href="/wiki/Thad_Allen" title="Thad Allen">Thad Allen</a> declared the well "effectively dead" and said that it posed no further threat to the Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-Aspress_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aspress-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recurrent_or_continued_leakage">Recurrent or continued leakage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Recurrent or continued leakage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3978" data-file-height="2323" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_DVIDSHUB_-_Oil_Spill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Discoverer Enterprise and the Q4000 work around the clock burning undesirable gases from the still uncapped Deepwater Horizon well in the Gulf of Mexico. 26 June 2010</div></div></div>
<p>In May 2010, BP admitted they had "discovered things that were broken in the sub-surface" during the "top kill" effort.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Oil slicks were reported in March<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc220311_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc220311-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and August 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera3_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera3-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> in March<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera1_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and October 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-98_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-98-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-99_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-99-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and in January 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-onwingsofcare_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-onwingsofcare-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Repeated scientific analyses confirmed that the sheen was a chemical match for oil from Macondo well.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-94_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-94-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-95_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-95-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The USCG initially said the oil was too dispersed to recover and posed no threat to the coastline,<sup id="cite_ref-nola010911_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola010911-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> but later warned BP and Transocean that they might be held financially responsible for cleaning up the new oil.<sup id="cite_ref-nola101012_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola101012-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> USGS director <a href="/wiki/Marcia_McNutt" title="Marcia McNutt">Marcia McNutt</a> stated that the riser pipe could hold at most 1,000 barrels (160&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) because it is open on both ends, making it unlikely to hold the amount of oil being observed.<sup id="cite_ref-bw111012_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw111012-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2012, BP reported that they had found and plugged leaking oil from the failed containment dome, now abandoned about 1,500 feet (460&#160;m) from the main well.<sup id="cite_ref-cbc171212_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbc171212-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cbc131212_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbc131212-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nola251012_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola251012-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In December 2012, the USCG conducted a subsea survey; no oil coming from the wells or the wreckage was found and its source remains unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramseur_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramseur-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nola281212_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola281212-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In addition, a white, milky substance was observed seeping from the wreckage. According to BP and the USCG, it is "not oil and it's not harmful."<sup id="cite_ref-cbs310113_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbs310113-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2013, BP said that they were continuing to investigate possible sources of the oil sheen. Chemical data implied that the substance might be residual oil leaking from the wreckage. If that proves to be the case, the sheen can be expected to eventually disappear. Another possibility is that it is formation oil escaping from the subsurface, using the Macondo well casing as flow conduit, possibly intersecting a naturally occurring fault, and then following that to escape at the surface some distance from the wellhead. If it proves to be oil from the subsurface, then that could indicate the possibility of an indefinite release of oil. The oil slick was comparable in size to <a href="/wiki/Cold_seeps" class="mw-redirect" title="Cold seeps">naturally occurring oil seeps</a> and was not large enough to pose an immediate threat to wildlife.<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera1_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NBC-Sheen-1-13_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBC-Sheen-1-13-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Containment.2C_collection_and_use_of_dispersants"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Containment,_collection_and_use_of_dispersants">Containment, collection and use of dispersants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Containment, collection and use of dispersants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill_response" title="Deepwater Horizon oil spill response">Deepwater Horizon oil spill response</a></div>
<p>The fundamental strategies for addressing the spill were containment, dispersal and removal.  In summer 2010, approximately 47,000&#160;people and 7,000&#160;vessels were involved in the project. By 3 October 2012, federal response costs amounted to $850&#160;million, mostly reimbursed by BP.  As of January 2013, 935&#160;personnel were still involved. By that time cleanup had cost BP over $14&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramseur_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramseur-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was estimated with plus-or-minus 10% uncertainty that 4.9 million barrels (780,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil was released from the well; 4.1 million barrels (650,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil went into the Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-153_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-153-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The report led by the Department of the Interior and the NOAA said that "75% [of oil] has been cleaned up by Man or Mother Nature"; however, only about 25% of released oil was collected or removed while about 75% of oil remained in the environment in one form or another.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-149_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-149-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, Markus Huettel, a <a href="/wiki/Benthic_zone" title="Benthic zone">benthic ecologist</a> at Florida State University, maintained that while much of BP's oil was degraded or evaporated, at least 60% remains unaccounted for.<sup id="cite_ref-ng220312_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ng220312-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2010, a local native set up a network for people to volunteer their assistance in cleaning up beaches. Boat captains were given the opportunity to offer the use of their boat to help clean and prevent the oil from further spreading. To assist with the efforts the captains had to register their ships with the Vessels of Opportunity, however an issue arose when more boats registered than actually participated in the clean up efforts - only a third of the registered boats. Many local supporters were disappointed with BP's slow response, prompting the formation of The Florida Key Environmental Coalition.  This coalition gained significant influence in the clean up of the oil spill to try and gain some control over the situation. <sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Containment">Containment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Containment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg/220px-Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg/330px-Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg/440px-Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tedx-oil-spill-0075.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil containment boom used in an attempt to protect barrier islands</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Boom_(containment)" title="Boom (containment)">Containment booms</a> stretching over 4,200,000 feet (1,300&#160;km) were deployed, either to corral the oil or as barriers to protect marshes, mangroves, shrimp/crab/oyster ranches or other ecologically sensitive areas. Booms extend 18–48 inches (0.46–1.22&#160;m) above and below the water surface and were effective only in relatively calm and slow-moving waters. Including one-time use <a href="/wiki/Sorbent" title="Sorbent">sorbent</a> booms, a total of 13,300,000 feet (4,100&#160;km) of booms were deployed.<sup id="cite_ref-butler_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Booms were criticized for washing up on the shore with the oil, allowing oil to escape above or below the boom, and for ineffectiveness in more than three to four-foot (90–120&#160;cm) waves.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-104_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-104-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-guardian190610_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian190610-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mnn090610_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mnn090610-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_barrier_island_plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Louisiana barrier island plan">Louisiana barrier island plan</a> was developed to construct <a href="/wiki/Barrier_island" title="Barrier island">barrier islands</a> to protect the coast of Louisiana. The plan was criticised for its expense and poor results.<sup id="cite_ref-sa080610_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sa080610-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pm220610_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pm220610-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Critics allege that the decision to pursue the project was political with little scientific input.<sup id="cite_ref-advocate110710_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-advocate110710-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The EPA expressed concern that the booms would threaten wildlife.<sup id="cite_ref-ap090910_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap090910-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For a time, a group called Matter of Trust, citing insufficient availability of manufactured oil absorption booms, campaigned to encourage hair salons, dog groomers and sheep farmers to donate hair, fur and wool clippings, stuffed in pantyhose or tights, to help contain oil near impacted shores, a technique dating back to the Exxon Valdez disater.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_human_hair_mats_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_human_hair_mats-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wash_Post_May_2010_hair_booms_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wash_Post_May_2010_hair_booms-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_Corexit_dispersant">Use of Corexit dispersant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Use of Corexit dispersant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large four propeller airplane sprays Corexit onto oil-sheen water" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg/220px-C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg/330px-C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg/440px-C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1397" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:C-130_support_oil_spill_cleanup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A C-130 Hercules sprays <a href="/wiki/Corexit" title="Corexit">Corexit</a> dispersant onto the Gulf of Mexico</div></div></div>
<p>The spill was also notable for the volume of <a href="/wiki/Corexit" title="Corexit">Corexit</a> <a href="/wiki/Oil_dispersants" title="Oil dispersants">oil dispersant</a> used and for application methods that were "purely experimental."<sup id="cite_ref-butler_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Altogether, 1.84&#160;million US gallons (7,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of dispersants were used; of this 771,000 US gallons (2,920&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) were released at the wellhead.<sup id="cite_ref-staff4_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-staff4-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Subsea injection had never previously been tried but due to the spill's unprecedented nature BP together with USCG and EPA decided to use it.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-120_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-120-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Over 400&#160;sorties were flown to release the product.<sup id="cite_ref-butler_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Although usage of dispersants was described as "the most effective and fast moving tool for minimizing shoreline impact",<sup id="cite_ref-butler_121-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> the approach continues to be investigated.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-113_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-113-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-114_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-114-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-115_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-115-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2011 analysis conducted by <a href="/wiki/Earthjustice" title="Earthjustice">Earthjustice</a> and Toxipedia showed that the dispersant could contain cancer-causing agents, hazardous toxins and <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_disruptor" title="Endocrine disruptor">endocrine-disrupting</a> chemicals.<sup id="cite_ref-pqarchiver_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pqarchiver-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Environmental scientists expressed concerns that the dispersants add to the toxicity of a spill, increasing the threat to <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a>. The dangers are even greater when poured into the source of a spill, because they are picked up by the current and wash through the Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-127_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-127-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> According to BP and federal officials, dispersant use stopped after the cap was in place;<sup id="cite_ref-Bolstad_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bolstad-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-139_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-139-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> however, marine toxicologist <a href="/wiki/Riki_Ott" title="Riki Ott">Riki Ott</a> wrote in an open letter to the EPA that Corexit use continued after that date<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-138_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-138-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Project" title="Government Accountability Project">GAP</a> investigation stated that "[a] majority of GAP witnesses cited indications that Corexit was used after [July 2010]."<sup id="cite_ref-GAP_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GAP-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a NALCO manual obtained by <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Project" title="Government Accountability Project">GAP</a>, Corexit 9527 is an “eye and skin irritant. Repeated or excessive exposure ... may cause injury to red blood cells (hemolysis), kidney or the liver.” The manual adds: “Excessive exposure may cause central nervous system effects, nausea, vomiting, anesthetic or narcotic effects.” It advises, “Do not get in eyes, on skin, on clothing,” and “Wear suitable protective clothing.” For Corexit 9500 the manual advised, “Do not get in eyes, on skin, on clothing,” “Avoid breathing vapor,” and “Wear suitable protective clothing.” According to <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">FOIA</a> requests obtained by GAP, neither the protective gear nor the manual were distributed to Gulf oil spill cleanup workers.
</p><p>Corexit EC9500A and Corexit EC9527A were the principal variants.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-116_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-116-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The two formulations are neither the least toxic, nor the most effective, among EPA's approved dispersants, but BP said it chose to use Corexit because it was available the week of the rig explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-csm0515_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csm0515-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-csm0517_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csm0517-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> On 19 May, the EPA gave BP 24&#160;hours to choose less toxic alternatives to Corexit from the National Contingency Plan Product Schedule, and begin applying them within 72 hours of EPA approval or provide a detailed reasoning why no approved products met the standards.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-130_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-130-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-131_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-131-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> On 20 May, BP determined that none of the alternative products met all three criteria of availability, non-toxicity and effectiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-133_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-133-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> On 24 May, EPA Administrator <a href="/wiki/Lisa_P._Jackson" title="Lisa P. Jackson">Lisa P. Jackson</a> ordered EPA to conduct its own evaluation of alternatives and ordered BP to reduce dispersant use by 75%.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-134_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-134-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-135_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-135-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> BP reduced Corexit use by 25,689 to 23,250 US gallons (97,240 to 88,010&#160;L) per day, a 9% decline.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-136_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-136-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> On 2 August 2010, the EPA said dispersants did no more harm to the environment than the oil and that they stopped a large amount of oil from reaching the coast by breaking it down faster.<sup id="cite_ref-Bolstad_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bolstad-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> However, some independent scientists and EPA's own experts continue to voice concerns about the approach.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Underwater injection of Corexit into the leak may have created the oil plumes which were discovered below the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-csm0517_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csm0517-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Because the dispersants were applied at depth, much of the oil never rose to the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-latimes1_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes1-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> One plume was 22 miles (35&#160;km) long, more than 1 mile (1,600&#160;m) wide and 650 feet (200&#160;m) deep.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-141_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-141-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In a major study on the plume, experts were most concerned about the slow pace at which the oil was breaking down in the cold, 40&#160;°F (4&#160;°C) water at depths of 3,000 feet (900&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-142_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-142-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 2012, a study from <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Tech" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgia Tech">Georgia Tech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universidad_Autonoma_de_Aguascalientes" class="mw-redirect" title="Universidad Autonoma de Aguascalientes">Universidad Autonoma de Aguascalientes</a> in Environmental Pollution journal reported that Corexit used during the BP oil spill had increased the <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> of the oil by 52 times.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>  The scientists concluded that "Mixing oil with dispersant increased toxicity to ecosystems" and made the gulf oil spill worse."<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal">Removal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Removal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg/220px-Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg" width="220" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg/330px-Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg/440px-Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="2982" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Skimming_Oil_in_Gulf_of_Mexico.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil skimming vessels (distance) in the Gulf of Mexico</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg/220px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg/330px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg/440px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4248" data-file-height="2832" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_photo_essay_100506-N-6070S-819.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dark clouds of smoke and fire emerge as oil burns during a controlled fire in the Gulf of Mexico, 6 May 2010</div></div></div>
<p>The three basic approaches for removing the oil from the water were: combustion, offshore filtration, and collection for later processing. USCG said 33&#160;million US gallons (120,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of tainted water was recovered, including 5&#160;million US gallons (19,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil. BP said 826,800 barrels (131,450&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) had been recovered or flared.<sup id="cite_ref-Schoof_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoof-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> It is calculated that about 5% of leaked oil was burned at the surface and 3% was skimmed.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-149_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-149-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> On the most demanding day 47,849 people were assigned on the response works.<sup id="cite_ref-report2011_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-report2011Live_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011Live-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From April to mid-July 2010, 411&#160;controlled <i>in-situ</i> fires remediated approximately 265,000 barrels (11.1&#160;million US&#160;gal; 42,100&#160;m<sup>3</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-butler_121-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The fires released small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a>, including cancer-causing <a href="/wiki/Dioxin" title="Dioxin">dioxins</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/EPA" class="mw-redirect" title="EPA">EPA</a>'s report, the released amount is not enough to pose an added cancer risk to workers and coastal residents, while a second research team concluded that there was only a small added risk.<sup id="cite_ref-ap121110_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap121110-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_(8743617563).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_%288743617563%29.jpg/220px-Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_%288743617563%29.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_%288743617563%29.jpg/330px-Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_%288743617563%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_%288743617563%29.jpg/440px-Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_%288743617563%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2980" data-file-height="2076" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beach_Clean_up_during_DWH_(8743617563).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workers cleaning a beach affected by the spill.</div></div></div>
<p>Oil was collected from water by using <a href="/wiki/Skimmer_(machine)" title="Skimmer (machine)">skimmers</a>. In total 2,063 various skimmers were used.<sup id="cite_ref-report2011_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-report2011Live_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011Live-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> For offshore, more than 60&#160;open-water skimmers were deployed, including 12&#160;purpose-built vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-butler_121-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> EPA regulations prohibited skimmers that left more than 15 parts per million (ppm) of oil in the water. Many large-scale skimmers exceeded the limit.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-144_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-144-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Due to use of <a href="/wiki/Corexit" title="Corexit">Corexit</a> the oil was too dispersed to collect, according to a spokesperson for shipowner <a href="/wiki/TMT_(Today_Makes_Tomorrow)" title="TMT (Today Makes Tomorrow)">TMT</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-148_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-148-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In mid-June 2010, BP ordered 32&#160;machines that <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_water-oil_separator" class="mw-redirect" title="Centrifugal water-oil separator">separate oil and water</a>, with each machine capable of extracting up to 2,000 barrels per day (320&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d).<sup id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes20100624_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes20100624-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> After one week of testing, BP began to proceed<sup id="cite_ref-abcnews1_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews1-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> and by 28 June, had removed 890,000 barrels (141,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-BP100626_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP100626-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the well was captured, the cleanup of shore became the main task of the response works. Two main types of affected coast were sandy beaches and <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a>. On beaches the main techniques were sifting sand, removing tar balls, and digging out tar mats manually or by using mechanical devices.<sup id="cite_ref-report2011_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-report2011Live_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report2011Live-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> For marshes, techniques such as vacuum and pumping, low-pressure flush, vegetation cutting, and <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a> were used.<sup id="cite_ref-butler_121-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butler-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil-eating_microbes">Oil-eating microbes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Oil-eating microbes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dispersants are said to facilitate the digestion of the oil by microbes. Mixing dispersants with oil at the wellhead would keep some oil below the surface and in theory, allowing <a href="/wiki/Microbe" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbe">microbes</a> to digest the oil before it reached the surface. Various risks were identified and evaluated, in particular that an increase in microbial activity might reduce subsea oxygen levels, threatening fish and other animals.<sup id="cite_ref-science735_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science735-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several studies suggest that microbes successfully consumed part of the oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramseur_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramseur-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-valentine_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valentine-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> By mid-September, other research claimed that microbes mainly digested natural gas rather than oil.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-158_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-158-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> David L. Valentine, a professor of microbial geochemistry at <a href="/wiki/UC_Santa_Barbara" class="mw-redirect" title="UC Santa Barbara">UC Santa Barbara</a>, said that the capability of microbes to break down the leaked oil had been greatly exaggerated.<sup id="cite_ref-news.yahoo.com_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.yahoo.com-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>  However, biogeochemist Chris Reddy, said natural microorganisms are a big reason why the oil spill in the Gulf of Mexico was not far worse.<sup id="cite_ref-MicrobesSA2015_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MicrobesSA2015-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MicrobesSA2010_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MicrobesSA2010-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetically modified">Genetically modified</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Alcanivorax_borkumensis" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcanivorax borkumensis">Alcanivorax borkumensis</a></i> was added to the waters to speed digestion.<sup id="cite_ref-news.yahoo.com_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.yahoo.com-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-160_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-160-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The delivery method of microbes to oil patches was proposed by the Russian <a href="/wiki/Research_and_Development_Institute_of_Ecology_and_the_Sustainable_Use_of_Natural_Resources" title="Research and Development Institute of Ecology and the Sustainable Use of Natural Resources">Research and Development Institute of Ecology and the Sustainable Use of Natural Resources</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Access_restrictions">Access restrictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Access restrictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 18 May 2010, BP was designated the lead "Responsible Party" under the <a href="/wiki/Oil_Pollution_Act_of_1990" title="Oil Pollution Act of 1990">Oil Pollution Act of 1990</a>, which meant that BP had operational authority in coordinating the response.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first video images were released on 12 May, and further video images were released by members of Congress who had been given access to them by BP.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimesLimits_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimesLimits-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
