</p><p>During the spill response operations, at the request of the Coast Guard, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> (FAA) implemented a 900-square-mile (2,300&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/Temporary_flight_restriction" class="mw-redirect" title="Temporary flight restriction">temporary flight restriction</a> zone over the operations area.<sup id="cite_ref-faa_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faa-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-airspacemag_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-airspacemag-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-weeklystandard_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weeklystandard-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Restrictions were to prevent civilian air traffic from interfering with aircraft assisting the response effort.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimesLimits_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimesLimits-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> All flights in the operations' area were prohibited except flight authorized by <a href="/wiki/Air_traffic_control" title="Air traffic control">air traffic control</a>; routine flights supporting offshore oil operations; federal, state, local and military flight operations supporting spill response; and air ambulance and law enforcement operations. Exceptions for these restrictions were granted on a case-by-case basis dependent on safety issues, operational requirements, weather conditions, and traffic volume. No flights, except aircraft conducting aerial chemical dispersing operations, or for landing and takeoff, were allowed below 1,000 metres (3,300&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-faa_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faa-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Notwithstanding restrictions, there were 800 to 1,000&#160;flights per day during the operations.<sup id="cite_ref-nola290510_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola290510-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Local and federal authorities citing BP's authority denied access to members of the press attempting to document the spill from the air, from boats, and on the ground, blocking access to areas that were open to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimesLimits_177-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimesLimits-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewsweekPress_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewsweekPress-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zak_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zak-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In some cases photographers were granted access only with BP officials escorting them on BP-contracted boats and aircraft. In one example, the U.S. Coast Guard stopped <a href="/wiki/Jean-Michel_Cousteau" title="Jean-Michel Cousteau">Jean-Michel Cousteau</a>'s boat and allowed it to proceed only after the Coast Guard was assured that no journalists were on board.<sup id="cite_ref-NewsweekPress_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewsweekPress-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In another example, a <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">CBS News</a> crew was denied access to the oil-covered beaches of the spill area. The CBS crew was told by the authorities: "this is BP's rules, not ours," when trying to film the area.<sup id="cite_ref-NewsweekPress_184-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewsweekPress-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Some members of Congress criticized the restrictions placed on access by journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimesLimits_177-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimesLimits-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The FAA denied that BP employees or contractors made decisions on flights and access, saying those decisions were made by the FAA and Coast Guard.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimesLimits_177-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimesLimits-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-weeklystandard_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weeklystandard-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The FAA acknowledged that media access was limited to hired planes or helicopters, but was arranged through the Coast Guard.<sup id="cite_ref-nola290510_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola290510-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The Coast Guard and BP denied having a policy of restricting journalists; they noted that members of the media had been embedded with the authorities and allowed to cover response efforts since the beginning of the effort, with more than 400 embeds aboard boats and aircraft to date.<sup id="cite_ref-Zak_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zak-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> They also said that they wanted to provide access to the information while maintaining safety.<sup id="cite_ref-Zak_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zak-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleanup">Cleanup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cleanup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 15 April 2014, BP claimed that cleanup along the coast was substantially complete, but the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a> responded by stating that a lot of work remained. Using physical barriers such as floating booms, cleanup workers’ objective was to keep the oil from spreading any further. They used skimmer boats to remove a majority of the oil and they used <a href="/wiki/Sorbent" title="Sorbent">sorbents</a> to absorb any remnant of oil like a sponge. Although that method did not remove the oil completely, chemicals called <a href="/wiki/Dispersant" title="Dispersant">dispersants</a> are used to hasten the oil’s degradation to prevent the oil from doing further damage to the marine habitats below the surface water. For the Deep Horizon oil spill, cleanup workers used 1,400,000 US gallons (5,300,000&#160;l; 1,200,000&#160;imp&#160;gal) of various chemical dispersants to further breakdown the oil.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The State of Louisiana was funded by BP to do regular testing of fish, shellfish, water, and sand. Initial testing regularly showed detectable levels of dioctyl sodium sulfosuccinate, a chemical used in the clean up. Testing over the past year reported by GulfSource.org, for the pollutants tested have not produced results.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">Consequences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Consequences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Environmental impact of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Environmental impact of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg/220px-Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg/330px-Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg/440px-Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Turtle_Rescue_and_Rehabilitation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Capturing heavily oiled young turtles 20 to 40 miles offshore for rehabilitation; 14 June 2010</div></div></div>
<p>The spill area hosts 8,332&#160;species, including more than 1,270&#160;fish, 604 <a href="/wiki/Polychaete" title="Polychaete">polychaetes</a>, 218&#160;birds, 1,456&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mollusks" class="mw-redirect" title="Mollusks">mollusks</a>, 1,503&#160;<a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, 4&#160;sea turtles and 29&#160;marine mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-165_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-165-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-166_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-166-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Between May and June 2010, the spill waters contained 40 times more <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs) than before the spill.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-174_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-174-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> PAHs are often linked to oil spills and include <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a> and chemicals that pose various health risks to humans and marine life. The PAHs were most concentrated near the Louisiana Coast, but levels also jumped 2–3 fold in areas off Alabama, Mississippi and Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-174_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-174-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> PAHs can harm marine species directly and <a href="/wiki/Microbe" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbe">microbes</a> used to consume the oil can reduce marine <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> levels.<sup id="cite_ref-collins_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collins-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>  The oil contained approximately 40% <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> by weight, compared to about 5% found in typical oil deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-methane_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-methane-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Methane can potentially suffocate marine life and create "dead zones" where oxygen is depleted.<sup id="cite_ref-methane_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-methane-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 study of the effects of the oil spill on <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a> funded by <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> (NOAA), <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Monterey_Bay_Aquarium" title="Monterey Bay Aquarium">Monterey Bay Aquarium</a> and published in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, found that the toxins from oil spills can cause irregular heartbeats leading to <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a>.  Calling the vicinity of the spill "one of the most productive ocean ecosystems in the world", the study found that even at very low concentrations "PAH cardiotoxicity was potentially a common form of injury among a broad range of species in the vicinity of the oil."<sup id="cite_ref-LATimes-fish-toxins_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-fish-toxins-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Another peer-reviewed study, released in March 2014 and conducted by 17 scientists from the United States and Australia and published in the <a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences</a>, found that tuna and <a href="/wiki/Amberjack" title="Amberjack">amberjack</a> that were exposed to oil from the spill developed deformities of the heart and other organs that would be expected to be fatal or at least life-shortening.
The scientists said that their findings would most likely apply to other large predator fish and "even to humans, whose developing hearts are in many ways similar."  BP responded that the concentrations of oil in the study were a level rarely seen in the Gulf, but <i>The New York Times</i> reported that the BP statement was contradicted by the study.<sup id="cite_ref-Wines-24-March-14_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wines-24-March-14-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oiled_Pelicans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Oiled_Pelicans.jpg/220px-Oiled_Pelicans.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Oiled_Pelicans.jpg/330px-Oiled_Pelicans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Oiled_Pelicans.jpg/440px-Oiled_Pelicans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oiled_Pelicans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An oiled <a href="/wiki/Brown_pelican" title="Brown pelican">brown pelican</a> near Grand Isle, Louisiana</div></div></div>
<p>The oil dispersant <a href="/wiki/Corexit" title="Corexit">Corexit</a>, previously only used as a surface application, was released underwater in unprecedented amounts, with the intent of making it more easily biodegraded by naturally occurring microbes.  Thus, oil that would normally rise to the surface of the water was <a href="/wiki/Emulsified" class="mw-redirect" title="Emulsified">emulsified</a> into tiny droplets and remained suspended in the water and on the sea floor.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>  The oil and dispersant mixture permeated the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a> through <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-174_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-174-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ortmann_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ortmann-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-187_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-187-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Signs of an oil-and-dispersant mix were found under the shells of tiny <a href="/wiki/Callinectes_sapidus" title="Callinectes sapidus">blue crab</a> larvae.<sup id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost1_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost1-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
A study of insect populations in the coastal marshes affected by the spill also found a significant impact.
<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-191_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-191-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Chemicals from the spill were found in migratory birds as far away as Minnesota. <a href="/wiki/Pelican" title="Pelican">Pelican</a> eggs contained "petroleum compounds and Corexit".<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-115_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-115-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Dispersant and PAHs from oil are believed to have caused "disturbing numbers" of <a href="/wiki/Mutated" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutated">mutated</a> fish that scientists and commercial fishers saw in 2012, including 50% of shrimp found lacking eyes and eye sockets.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-197_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-197-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-196_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-196-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Fish with oozing sores and lesions were first noted by fishermen in November 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera2_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera2-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the spill, approximately 0.1% of Gulf fish had lesions or sores. A report from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> said that many locations showed 20% of fish with lesions, while later estimates reached 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera2_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera2-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> In October 2013, <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a> reported that the gulf ecosystem was "in crisis", citing a decline in seafood catches, as well as deformities and lesions found in fish.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2010 it was reported that the spill was "already having a 'devastating' effect on marine life in the Gulf".<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-195_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-195-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>  Damage to the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_floor" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocean floor">ocean floor</a> especially endangered the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_pancake_batfish" title="Louisiana pancake batfish">Louisiana pancake batfish</a> whose range is entirely contained within the spill-affected area.<sup id="cite_ref-fish2fork_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fish2fork-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>  In March 2012, a definitive link was found between the death of a <a href="/wiki/Deep-water_coral" title="Deep-water coral">Gulf coral community</a> and the spill.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-188_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-188-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-189_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-189-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-190_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-190-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abc031210_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abc031210-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> According to NOAA, a <a href="/wiki/Cetacean" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetacean">cetacean</a> Unusual Mortality Event (UME) has been recognized since before the spill began, NOAA is investigating possible contributing factors to the ongoing UME from the Deepwater Horizon spill, with the possibility of eventual criminal charges being filed if the spill is shown to be connected.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-13_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-13-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Some estimates are that only 2% of the carcasses of killed mammals have been recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-183_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-183-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Striped_Dolphins.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Striped_Dolphins.jpg/220px-Striped_Dolphins.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Striped_Dolphins.jpg/330px-Striped_Dolphins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Striped_Dolphins.jpg/440px-Striped_Dolphins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1290" data-file-height="860" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Striped_Dolphins.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Striped dolphins (<i><a href="/wiki/Stenella_coeruleoalba" class="mw-redirect" title="Stenella coeruleoalba">Stenella coeruleoalba</a></i>) observed in emulsified oil on 29 April 2010</div></div></div> 
<p>In the first birthing season for dolphins after the spill, dead baby dolphins washed up along Mississippi and Alabama shorelines at about 10&#160;times the normal number.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-181_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-181-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since October 2016">dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup> A peer-reviewed NOAA/BP study disclosed that nearly half the bottlenose dolphins tested in mid-2011 in Barataria Bay, a heavily oiled area, were in “guarded or worse” condition, "including 17 percent that were not expected to survive". BP officials deny that the disease conditions are related to the spill, saying that dolphin deaths actually began being reported before the BP oil spill.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-191_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-191-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-186_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-186-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> By 2013, over 650 dolphins had been found stranded in the oil spill area, a four-fold increase over the historical average.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/National_Wildlife_Federation" title="National Wildlife Federation">National Wildlife Federation</a> (NWF) reports that sea turtles, mostly endangered <a href="/wiki/Kemp%E2%80%99s_ridley_sea_turtle" class="mw-redirect" title="Kemp’s ridley sea turtle">Kemp’s ridley sea turtles</a>, have been stranding at a high rate.  Before the spill there were an average of 100 strandings per year; since the spill the number has jumped to roughly 500.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
NWF senior scientist Doug Inkley notes that the marine death rates are unprecedented and occurring high in the food chain, strongly suggesting there is "something amiss with the Gulf ecosystem".<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> In December 2013, the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Environmental_Science_%26_Technology" title="Environmental Science &amp; Technology">Environmental Science &amp; Technology</a></i> published a study finding that of 32 <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> briefly captured from 24-km stretch near southeastern Louisiana, half were seriously ill or dying. BP said the report was “inconclusive as to any causation associated with the spill”.<sup id="cite_ref-dolphins-huffpo-2-14_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dolphins-huffpo-2-14-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Independent-dolphins-2-14_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Independent-dolphins-2-14-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bay_Jimmy,_Plaquemines_Parish_(9152010).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Bay_Jimmy%2C_Plaquemines_Parish_%289152010%29.jpg/220px-Bay_Jimmy%2C_Plaquemines_Parish_%289152010%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Bay_Jimmy%2C_Plaquemines_Parish_%289152010%29.jpg/330px-Bay_Jimmy%2C_Plaquemines_Parish_%289152010%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Bay_Jimmy%2C_Plaquemines_Parish_%289152010%29.jpg/440px-Bay_Jimmy%2C_Plaquemines_Parish_%289152010%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bay_Jimmy,_Plaquemines_Parish_(9152010).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heavy oiling of Bay Jimmy, Plaquemines Parish; 15 September 2010</div></div></div>
<p>In 2012, tar balls continued to wash up along the Gulf coast<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-14_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-14-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nola1012_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola1012-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nola0911_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola0911-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wdsu040912_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wdsu040912-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> and in 2013, tar balls could still be found in on the Mississippi and Louisiana coasts, along with oil sheens in marshes and signs of severe erosion of coastal islands, brought about by the death of trees and marsh grass from exposure to the oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Dermansky_4-20-13_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dermansky_4-20-13-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>  In 2013, former NASA physicist Bonny Schumaker noted a "dearth of marine life" in a radius 30 to 50 miles (48 to 80&#160;km) around the well, after flying over the area numerous times since May 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, researchers found that oil on the bottom of the seafloor did not seem to be degrading,<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>  and observed a phenomenon called a "dirty blizzard": oil in the water column began clumping around suspended sediments, and falling to the ocean floor in an "underwater rain of oily particles." The result could have long-term effects because oil could remain in the food chain for generations.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 bluefin tuna study in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> found that oil already broken down by wave action and chemical dispersants was more toxic than fresh oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Australian_-_2014_bluefin_study_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Australian_-_2014_bluefin_study-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> A 2015 study of the relative toxicity of oil and dispersants to coral also found that the dispersants were more toxic than the oil.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2015 study by the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a>, published in <i><a href="/wiki/PLOS_ONE" class="mw-redirect" title="PLOS ONE">PLOS ONE</a>,</i> links the sharp increase in dolphin deaths to the <i>Deepwater Horizon</i> oil spill.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 April 2016, a research team reported that 88 percent of about 360 baby or <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillborn</a> dolphins within the spill area "had abnormal or under-developed lungs", compared to 15 percent in other areas. The study was published in the April 2016 <i> Diseases of Aquatic Organisms</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_consequences">Health consequences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Health consequences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_consequences_of_the_Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Health consequences of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Health consequences of the Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a></div>
<p>By June 2010, 143&#160;spill-exposure cases had been reported to the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Department_of_Health_and_Hospitals" title="Louisiana Department of Health and Hospitals">Louisiana Department of Health and Hospitals</a>; 108 of those involved workers in the clean-up efforts, while 35 were reported by residents.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-230_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-230-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Chemicals from the oil and dispersant are believed to be the cause; it is believed that the addition of dispersants made the oil more toxic.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg/220px-Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg/330px-Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg/440px-Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2623" data-file-height="3949" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_waste_clean_up_at_Elmer%27s_Island_2010-05-21.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A worker cleans up oily waste on Elmer's Island just west of Grand Isle, La., 21 May 2010</div></div></div>
<p>The United States <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Health and Human Services">Department of Health and Human Services</a> set up the <a href="/wiki/GuLF_Study" title="GuLF Study">GuLF Study</a> in June 2010 in response to these reports. The study is run by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Environmental_Health_Sciences" title="National Institute of Environmental Health Sciences">National Institute of Environmental Health Sciences</a>, and will last at least five years.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera291010_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera291010-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG/220px-HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG/330px-HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG/440px-HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3721" data-file-height="2471" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HSE_workers_clean_up_Port_Fourchon_beach_2010-05-23.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workers contracted by BP clean up oil on a beach in Port Fourchon, Louisiana, 23 May 2010</div></div></div>
<p>Mike Robicheux, a Louisiana physician, described the situation as "the biggest public health crisis from a chemical poisoning in the history of this country."<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera170511_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera170511-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> In July, after testing the blood of BP cleanup workers and residents in Louisiana, Mississippi, Alabama, and Florida for <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a>, environmental scientist <a href="/wiki/Wilma_Subra" title="Wilma Subra">Wilma Subra</a> said she was "finding amounts 5 to 10 times in excess of the 95th percentile"; she said that "the presence of these chemicals in the blood indicates exposure."<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera291010_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera291010-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-democracynow070710_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-democracynow070710-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Riki_Ott" title="Riki Ott">Riki Ott</a>, a marine toxicologist with experience of the <a href="/wiki/Exxon_Valdez_oil_spill" title="Exxon Valdez oil spill">Exxon Valdez oil spill</a>, advised families to evacuate the Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-Aguilar22July_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aguilar22July-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> She said that workers from the Valdez spill had suffered long-term health consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the 26 May 2010 hospitalization of seven fishermen that were working in the cleanup crew, BP requested that the National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health perform a Health Hazard Evaluation. This was to cover all offshore cleanup activities, BP later requested a second NIOSH investigation of onshore cleanup operations. Tests for chemical exposure in the seven fishermen were negative; NIOSH concluded that the hospitalizations were most likely a result of heat, fatigue, and terpenes that were being used to clean the decks. Review of 10 later hospitalizations found that heat exposure and dehydration were consistent findings but could not establish chemical exposure. NIOSH personnel performed air monitoring around cleanup workers at sea, on land, and during the application of Corexit. Air concentrations of volatile organic compounds and PAHs never exceeded permissible exposure levels. A limitation of their methodology was that some VOCs may have already evaporated from the oil before they began their investigation. In their report, they suggest the possibility that respiratory symptoms might have been caused by high levels of ozone or reactive aldehydes in the air, possibly produced from photochemical reactions in the oil. NIOSH did note that many of the personnel involved were not donning personal protective equipment (gloves and impermeable coveralls) as they had been instructed to and emphasized that this was important protection against transdermal absorption of chemicals from the oil. Heat stress was found to be the most pressing safety concern.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Workers reported that they were not allowed to use respirators, and that their jobs were threatened if they did.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> OSHA said "cleanup workers are receiving "minimal" exposure to airborne toxins...OSHA will require that BP provide certain protective clothing, but not respirators."<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> <i>ProPublica</i> reported that workers were being photographed while working with no protective clothing.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> An independent investigation for <a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a> showed that BP did not hand out the legally required safety manual for use with Corexit, and were not provided with safety training or protective gear.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2012 survey of the health effects of the spill on cleanup workers reported "eye, nose and throat irritation; respiratory problems; blood in urine, vomit and rectal bleeding; seizures; nausea and violent vomiting episodes that last for hours; skin irritation, burning and lesions; short-term memory loss and confusion; liver and kidney damage; central nervous system effects and nervous system damage; hypertension; and miscarriages". Dr. James Diaz, writing for the <i>American Journal of Disaster Medicine</i>, said these ailments appearing in the Gulf reflected those reported after previous oil spills, like the Exxon Valdez. Diaz warned that "chronic adverse health effects, including cancers, liver and kidney disease, mental health disorders, birth defects and developmental disorders should be anticipated among sensitive populations and those most heavily exposed". Diaz also believes neurological disorders should be expected.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-239_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-239-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two years after the spill, a study initiated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> found <a href="/wiki/Biomarker_(medicine)" title="Biomarker (medicine)">biomarkers</a> matching the oil from the spill in the bodies of cleanup workers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Other studies have reported a variety of mental health issues, skin problems, breathing issues, coughing, and headaches.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, during the three-day "Gulf of Mexico Oil Spill &amp; Ecosystem Science Conference",<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> findings discussed included a '"significant percentage" of Gulf residents reporting mental health problems like anxiety, depression and <a href="/wiki/PTSD" class="mw-redirect" title="PTSD">PTSD</a>. These studies also showed that the bodies of former spill cleanup workers carry biomarkers of "many chemicals contained in the oil".<sup id="cite_ref-nola220113_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola220113-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study that investigated the health effects among children in Louisiana and Florida living less than 10 miles from the coast found that more than a third of the parents reported physical or mental health symptoms among their children. The parents reported "unexplained symptoms among their children, including bleeding ears, nose bleeds, and the early start of menstruation among girls," according to David Abramson, director of Columbia University's National Center for Disaster Preparedness.<sup id="cite_ref-nola220113_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nola220113-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A cohort study of almost 2200 Louisiana women found "high physical/environmental exposure was significantly associated with all 13 of the physical health symptoms surveyed, with the strongest associations for burning in nose, throat or lungs&#160;; sore throat; dizziness and wheezing. Women who suffered a high degree of economic disruption as a result of spill were significantly more likely to report wheezing; headaches; watery, burning, itchy eyes and stuffy, itchy, runny nose.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_and_political_consequences_of_the_Deepwater_Horizon_disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic and political consequences of the Deepwater Horizon disaster">Economic and political consequences of the Deepwater Horizon disaster</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG/220px-Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG/330px-Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG/440px-Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Orange_Beach_Do_Not_Swim_sign_walkway.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sign in <a href="/wiki/Orange_Beach,_Alabama" title="Orange Beach, Alabama">Orange Beach, Alabama</a> advising against swimming due to the oil spill</div></div></div>
<p>The spill had a strong economic impact to BP <sup id="cite_ref-pracale_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pracale-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> and also the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Gulf Coast">Gulf Coast</a>'s economy sectors such as offshore drilling, fishing and tourism. Estimates of lost tourism dollars were projected to cost the Gulf coastal economy up to 22.7 billion through 2013. In addition, Louisiana reported that lost visitor spending through the end of 2010 totaled $32 million, and losses through 2013 were expected to total $153 million in this state alone.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> The Gulf of Mexico commercial fishing industry was estimated to have lost $247 million as a result of postspill fisheries closures. One study projects that the overall impact of lost or degraded commercial, recreational, and mariculture fisheries in the Gulf could be $8.7 billion by 2020, with a potential loss of 22,000 jobs over the same time frame.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> BP's expenditures on the spill included the cost of the spill response, containment, relief well drilling, grants to the Gulf states, claims paid, and federal costs, including fines and penalties.<sup id="cite_ref-BP_costs_for_oil_spill_response_pass_$3_billion_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP_costs_for_oil_spill_response_pass_$3_billion-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> As of March 2012, BP estimated the company's total spill-related expenses do not exceed $37.2 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-settleAP_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-settleAP-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> However, by some estimations penalties that BP may be required to pay have reached as high as $90&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-huffington151112_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffington151112-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> In addition, in November 2012 the EPA announced that BP will be temporarily banned from seeking new contracts with the US government.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-21_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-21-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Due to the loss of the market value, BP had dropped from the second to the fourth largest of the four major oil companies by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> During the crisis, BP gas stations in the United States reported a sales drop of between 10 and 40% due to backlash against the company.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-224_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-224-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Local officials in Louisiana expressed concern that the offshore drilling moratorium imposed in response to the spill would further harm the economies of coastal communities as the oil industry directly or indirectly employs about 318,000 Louisiana residents (17% of all jobs in the state).<sup id="cite_ref-CSM_Sasser_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSM_Sasser-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> NOAA had closed 86,985 square miles (225,290&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), or approximately 36% of Federal waters in the Gulf of Mexico, for commercial fishing causing $2.5&#160;billion cost for the fishing industry.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-202_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-202-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-203_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-203-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fishing1_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fishing1-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> The U.S. Travel Association estimated that the economic impact of the oil spill on tourism across the Gulf Coast over a three-year period could exceed approximately $23 billion, in a region that supports over 400,000&#160;travel industry jobs generating $34 billion in revenue annually.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-219_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-219-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-220_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-220-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offshore_drilling_policies">Offshore drilling policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Offshore drilling policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_offshore_drilling_debate" title="United States offshore drilling debate">United States offshore drilling debate</a> and <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_deepwater_drilling_moratorium" title="2010 United States deepwater drilling moratorium">2010 United States deepwater drilling moratorium</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hornbeck_Offshore_Services_LLC_v._Salazar" title="Hornbeck Offshore Services LLC v. Salazar">Hornbeck Offshore Services LLC v. Salazar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/200px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png" width="200" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/300px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/400px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US oil production and imports, 1910–2012.</div></div></div>
<p>On 30 April 2010 President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> ordered the federal government to hold the issuing of new offshore drilling leases and authorized investigation of 29&#160;oil rigs in the Gulf in an effort to determine the cause of the disaster.<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg010510_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg010510-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CBS/AP_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBS/AP-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Later a six-month offshore drilling (below 500 feet (150&#160;m) of water) moratorium was enforced by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">United States Department of the Interior</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream240610_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream240610-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> The moratorium suspended work on 33&#160;rigs,<sup id="cite_ref-upstream240610_276-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream240610-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> and a group of affected companies formed the <a href="/wiki/Back_to_Work_Coalition" title="Back to Work Coalition">Back to Work Coalition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KATC_web_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KATC_web-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> On 22 June, a <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_judge" title="United States federal judge">United States federal judge</a> on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_Eastern_District_of_Louisiana" title="United States District Court for the Eastern District of Louisiana">United States District Court for the Eastern District of Louisiana</a> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Leach-Cross_Feldman" title="Martin Leach-Cross Feldman">Martin Leach-Cross Feldman</a> when ruling in the case <i><a href="/wiki/Hornbeck_Offshore_Services_LLC_v._Salazar" title="Hornbeck Offshore Services LLC v. Salazar">Hornbeck Offshore Services LLC v. Salazar</a></i>, lifted the moratorium finding it too broad, arbitrary and not adequately justified.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream240610_276-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream240610-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> The ban was lifted in October 2010.
</p><p>On 28 April 2010, the <a href="/wiki/National_Energy_Board" title="National Energy Board">National Energy Board</a> of Canada, which regulates offshore drilling in the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Arctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Arctic">Canadian Arctic</a> and along the <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia_Coast" title="British Columbia Coast">British Columbia Coast</a>, issued a letter to oil companies asking them to explain their argument against safety rules which require same-season relief wells.<sup id="cite_ref-canadian_regulator_response_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canadian_regulator_response-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> On 3 May California Governor <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a> withdrew his support for a proposed plan to allow expanded <a href="/wiki/Offshore_oil_and_gas_in_California" title="Offshore oil and gas in California">offshore drilling projects in California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CSM20100503_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSM20100503-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC20100503_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20100503-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> On 8 July, Florida Governor <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Crist" title="Charlie Crist">Charlie Crist</a> called for a special session of the state legislature to draft an amendment to the state constitution banning offshore drilling in state waters, which the legislature rejected on 20 July.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-242_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-242-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-243_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-243-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2011, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">United States Department of the Interior</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Minerals_Management_Service" title="Minerals Management Service">Minerals Management Service</a> was dissolved after it was determined it had exercised poor oversight over the drilling industry.  Three new agencies replaced it, separating the regulation, leasing, and revenue collection responsibilities respectively, among the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Safety_and_Environmental_Enforcement" title="Bureau of Safety and Environmental Enforcement">Bureau of Safety and Environmental Enforcement</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Ocean_Energy_Management" title="Bureau of Ocean Energy Management">Bureau of Ocean Energy Management</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Natural_Resources_Revenue" title="Office of Natural Resources Revenue">Office of Natural Resources Revenue</a>.
</p><p>In March 2014, BP was again allowed to bid for oil and gas leases.<sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_and_Schwartz_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson_and_Schwartz-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions">Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reactions_to_the_Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Reactions to the Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Reactions to the Deepwater Horizon oil spill</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._reactions">U.S. reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: U.S. reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG/220px-NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG/330px-NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG/440px-NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Take_Tar_From_Feathers.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protesters advocating boycott of BP</div></div></div>
<p>On 30 April President Obama dispatched the Secretaries of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_the_Interior" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of the Interior">Department of Interior</a> and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Homeland_Security" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Homeland Security">Homeland Security</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/EPA_Administrator" class="mw-redirect" title="EPA Administrator">EPA Administrator</a> and NOAA to the Gulf Coast to assess the disaster.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-278_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-278-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> In his 15 June speech, Obama said, "This oil spill is the worst environmental disaster America has ever faced... Make no mistake: we will fight this spill with everything we've got for as long as it takes. We will make BP pay for the damage their company has caused. And we will do whatever's necessary to help the Gulf Coast and its people recover from this tragedy."<sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup>  Interior Secretary Ken Salazar stated, "Our job basically is to keep the boot on the neck of British Petroleum."<sup id="cite_ref-Rand_Paul_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rand_Paul-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> Some observers suggested that the Obama administration was being overly aggressive in its criticisms, which some BP investors saw as an attempt to deflect criticism of his own handling of the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-287_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-287-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rand_Paul" title="Rand Paul">Rand Paul</a> accused President Obama of being anti-business and "un-American".<sup id="cite_ref-Rand_Paul_286-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rand_Paul-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Public opinion polls in the U.S. were generally critical of the way President Obama and the federal government handled the disaster and they were extremely critical of BPs response. Across the US, thousands participated in dozens of protests at BP gas stations and other locations,<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-279_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-279-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-280_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-280-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-281_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-281-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> reducing sales at some stations by 10% to 40%.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-282_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-282-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Industry claimed that disasters are infrequent and that this spill was an isolated incident and rejected claims of a loss of industry credibility.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-284_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-284-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Petroleum_Institute" title="American Petroleum Institute">American Petroleum Institute</a> (API) stated that the offshore drilling industry is important to job creation and economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-284_292-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-284-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-283_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-283-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> CEOs from the top five oil companies all agreed to work harder at improving safety.  API announced the creation of an offshore safety institute, separate from API's lobbying operation.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-285_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-285-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG/180px-NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG" width="180" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG/270px-NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG/360px-NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NOLA_BP_Oil_Flood_Protest_Union_Jack_stand.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American protester stands on a <a href="/wiki/Union_Jack" title="Union Jack">Union Jack</a>, presumably associating BP with the United Kingdom</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_International_Investment" title="Organization for International Investment">Organization for International Investment</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington D.C.</a>-based advocate for overseas investment in the United States, warned that the heated rhetoric was potentially damaging the reputation of British companies with operations in the United States and could spark a wave of U.S. protectionism that would restrict British firms from government contracts, political donations and lobbying.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian140710_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian140710-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-286_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-286-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2010, President Obama issued an executive order, specifically citing the Deepwater Horizon oil spill, that adopted recommendations from the Interagency Ocean Policy Task Force and established the National Ocean Council.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> The council called together a number of federal committees and departments engaged in ocean issues to work with a newly established committee for conservation and resource management.<sup id="cite_ref-bglobe_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bglobe-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> In June 2018, the executive order establishing the National Ocean Council was revoked by President Trump in an effort to roll back bureaucracy and "benefit ocean industries employ millions of Americans."<sup id="cite_ref-bglobe_298-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bglobe-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom_Reactions">United Kingdom Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: United Kingdom Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the UK, there was anger at the American press and news outlets for the misuse of the term "British Petroleum" for the company – a name which has not been used since British Petroleum merged with the American company <a href="/wiki/Amoco" title="Amoco">Amoco</a> in 1998 to form BP. It was said that the U.S. was 'dumping' the blame onto the British people and there were calls for British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> to protect British interests in the United States.  British pension fund managers (who have large holdings of BP shares and rely upon its dividends) accepted that while BP had to pay compensation for the spill and the environmental damage, they argued that the cost to the company's market value from President Obama's criticism was far outweighing the direct clean-up costs.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-287_287-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-287-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially BP downplayed the incident; its CEO <a href="/wiki/Tony_Hayward" title="Tony Hayward">Tony Hayward</a> called the amount of oil and dispersant "relatively tiny" in comparison with the "very big ocean."<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-288_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-288-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> Later, he drew an outpouring of criticism when he said that the spill was a disruption to Gulf Coast residents and himself adding, "You know, I'd like my life back."<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-289_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-289-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> BP's chief operating officer <a href="/wiki/Doug_Suttles" title="Doug Suttles">Doug Suttles</a> contradicted the underwater plume discussion noting, "It may be down to how you define what a plume is here… The oil that has been found is in very minute quantities."<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-290_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-290-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> In June, BP launched a PR campaign and successfully bid for several search terms related to the spill on Google and other search engines so that the first sponsored search result linked directly to the company's website.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-291_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-291-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-292_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-292-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> On 26 July 2010, it was announced that CEO Tony Hayward was to resign and would be replaced by <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dudley" title="Bob Dudley">Bob Dudley</a>, who is an American citizen and previously worked for Amoco.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-293_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-293-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-294_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-294-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hayward's involvement in Deepwater Horizon has left him a highly controversial public figure. In May 2013, he was honored as a "distinguished leader" by the University of Birmingham, but his award ceremony was stopped on multiple occasions by jeers and walk-outs and the focus of a protest from <a href="/wiki/People_%26_Planet" title="People &amp; Planet">People &amp; Planet</a> members.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2013, Hayward was awarded an honorary degree from <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gordon_University" title="Robert Gordon University">Robert Gordon University</a>. This was described as "a very serious error of judgement" by Friends of the Earth Scotland, and "a sick joke" by the university's Student President.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_reactions">International reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: International reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The U.S. government rejected offers of cleanup help from Canada, Croatia, France, Germany, Ireland, Mexico, the Netherlands, Norway, Romania, South Korea, Spain, Sweden, the United Kingdom, and the United Nations. 
The U.S. State Department listed 70 assistance offers from 23 countries, all being initially declined, but later, 8 had been accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-stpdf_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stpdf-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-295_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-295-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> The USCG actively requested skimming boats and equipment from several countries.<sup id="cite_ref-csmhelp_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csmhelp-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_aspects_and_settlements">Legal aspects and settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Legal aspects and settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investigations">Investigations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Investigations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon_investigation" title="Deepwater Horizon investigation">Deepwater Horizon investigation</a></div>
<p>In the United States the <i>Deepwater Horizon</i> investigation included several investigations and commissions, including reports by the USCG National Incident Commander, Admiral <a href="/wiki/Thad_Allen" title="Thad Allen">Thad Allen</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Commission_on_the_BP_Deepwater_Horizon_Oil_Spill_and_Offshore_Drilling" title="National Commission on the BP Deepwater Horizon Oil Spill and Offshore Drilling">National Commission on the BP Deepwater Horizon Oil Spill and Offshore Drilling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Ocean_Energy_Management,_Regulation_and_Enforcement" class="mw-redirect" title="Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, Regulation and Enforcement">Bureau of Ocean Energy Management, Regulation and Enforcement</a> (BOEMRE), <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Engineering" title="National Academy of Engineering">National Academy of Engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Research Council">National Research Council</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a>, National Oil Spill Commission, and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_Safety_and_Hazard_Investigation_Board" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical Safety and Hazard Investigation Board">Chemical Safety and Hazard Investigation Board</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramseur_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramseur-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The Republic of the Marshall Islands Maritime Administrator conducted a separate investigation on the marine casualty.<sup id="cite_ref-MHL_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MHL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> BP conducted its internal investigation.
</p><p>An investigation of the possible causes of the explosion was launched on 22 April 2010 by the USCG and the <a href="/wiki/Minerals_Management_Service" title="Minerals Management Service">Minerals Management Service</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream220410_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream220410-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> On 11 May the United States administration requested the National Academy of Engineering conduct an independent technical investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-doi110510_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi110510-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> The National Commission on the BP Deepwater Horizon Oil Spill and Offshore Drilling was established on 22 May to "consider the root causes of the disaster and offer options on safety and environmental precautions."<sup id="cite_ref-wh220510_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wh220510-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> The investigation by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">United States Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Holder" title="Eric Holder">Eric Holder</a> was announced on 1 June 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Holder-06-01_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holder-06-01-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> Also the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Energy_and_Commerce" title="United States House Committee on Energy and Commerce">United States House Committee on Energy and Commerce</a> conducted a number of hearings, including hearings of Tony Hayward and heads of Anadarko and Mitsui's exploration unit.<sup id="cite_ref-upstream170610_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream170610-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-upstream090710a_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upstream090710a-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> According to the US Congressional investigation, the rig's <a href="/wiki/Blowout_preventer" title="Blowout preventer">blowout preventer</a>, built by <a href="/wiki/Cameron_International_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cameron International Corporation">Cameron International Corporation</a>, had a hydraulic leak and a failed battery, and therefore failed.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_13/5_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_13/5-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 8 September 2010, BP released a 193-page report on its web site. The report places some of the blame for the accident on BP but also on <a href="/wiki/Halliburton" title="Halliburton">Halliburton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transocean" title="Transocean">Transocean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> The report found that on 20 April 2010, managers misread pressure data and gave their approval for rig workers to replace drilling fluid in the well with seawater, which was not heavy enough to prevent gas that had been leaking into the well from firing up the pipe to the rig, causing the explosion. The conclusion was that BP was partly to blame, as was Transocean, which owned the rig.<sup id="cite_ref-dailymail1_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailymail1-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> Responding to the report, Transocean and Halliburton placed all blame on BP.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 9 November 2010, a report by the Oil Spill Commission said that there had been "a rush to completion" on the well and criticised poor management decisions. "There was not a culture of safety on that rig," the co-chair said.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The National Commission on the BP Deepwater Horizon Oil Spill and Offshore Drilling released a final report on 5 January 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-telegraph-finalreport_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-finalreport-321">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-osc-final-report_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osc-final-report-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> The panel found that BP, Halliburton, and Transocean had attempted to work more cheaply and thus helped to trigger the explosion and ensuing leakage.<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc-finalreport_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc-finalreport-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> The report stated that "whether purposeful or not, many of the decisions that BP, Halliburton, and Transocean made that increased the risk of the Macondo blowout clearly saved those companies significant time (and money)."<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc-finalreport_323-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc-finalreport-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> BP released a statement in response to this, saying, that "even prior to the conclusion of the commission's investigation, BP instituted significant changes designed to further strengthen safety and risk management."<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-finalreport_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-finalreport-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Transocean, however, blamed BP for making the decisions before the actual explosion occurred and government officials for permitting those decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-wpost-finalreport_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wpost-finalreport-325">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> Halliburton stated that it was acting only upon the orders of BP when it injected the cement into the wall of the well.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-finalreport_324-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-finalreport-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-startribune-finalreport_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-startribune-finalreport-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> It criticized BP for its failure to run a <a href="/wiki/Cement_bond_log" title="Cement bond log">cement bond log</a> test.<sup id="cite_ref-wpost-finalreport_325-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wpost-finalreport-325">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> In the report, BP was accused of nine faults.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-finalreport_324-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-finalreport-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-startribune-finalreport_326-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-startribune-finalreport-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> One was that it had not used a diagnostic tool to test the strength of the cement.<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc-finalreport_323-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc-finalreport-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> Another was ignoring a pressure test that had failed.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-finalreport_324-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-finalreport-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Still another was for not plugging the pipe with cement.<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc-finalreport_323-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc-finalreport-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> The study did not, however, place the blame on any one of these events. Rather, it concluded that "notwithstanding these inherent risks, the accident of April 20 was avoidable" and that "it resulted from clear mistakes made in the first instance by BP, Halliburton and Transocean, and by government officials who, relying too much on industry's assertions of the safety of their operations, failed to create and apply a program of regulatory oversight that would have properly minimized the risk of deepwater drilling."<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-finalreport_324-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-finalreport-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-startribune-finalreport_326-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-startribune-finalreport-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> The panel also noted that the government regulators did not have sufficient knowledge or authority to notice these cost-cutting decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc-finalreport_323-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc-finalreport-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 March 2011, BOEMRE (former MMS) and the USCG published a forensic examination report on the blowout preventer, prepared by <a href="/wiki/Det_Norske_Veritas" class="mw-redirect" title="Det Norske Veritas">Det Norske Veritas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dnv230311_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnv230311-327">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> The report concluded that the primary cause of failure was that the blind shear rams failed to fully close and seal due to a portion of drill pipe buckling between the shearing blocks.
</p><p>The US government report issued in September 2011 stated that BP is ultimately responsible for the spill, and that Halliburton and Transocean share some of the blame.<sup id="cite_ref-BOERMEPR_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOERMEPR-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoBB-296_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoBB-296-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> The report states that the main cause was the defective cement job, and Halliburton, BP and Transocean were, in different ways, responsible for the accident.<sup id="cite_ref-BOERMEPR_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOERMEPR-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The report stated that, although the events leading to the sinking of Deepwater Horizon were set into motion by the failure to prevent a well blowout, the investigation revealed numerous systems deficiencies, and acts and omissions by Transocean and its <i>Deepwater Horizon</i> crew, that had an adverse impact on the ability to prevent or limit the magnitude of the disaster.  The report also states that a central cause of the blowout was failure of a cement barrier allowing hydrocarbons to flow up the wellbore, through the riser and onto the rig, resulting in the blowout.
